  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c105f96d-7175-4131-9cea-2a92755f96f2'
      });

  <span id="freeText5626540222384137714" style="display:none">11 CDs / 12.75 Hours <br /><br />Book Four finds Morgaine moving closer to the fate that will set her intractably against Arthur--her lover, brother, and now, enemy. Returning to Camelot during the Feast of Pentecost, Morgaine accuses Arthur of compromising the crown, and demands that he return Excalibur to her. When he refuses, Morgaine arranges a confrontation between her lover, Accolon, and Arthur in the kingdom of Fairy, resulting in Accolons death. Grieving and still without Excalibur, Morgaine makes a hasty retreat to Avalon. When she finally returns to Camelot, it is to retrieve Avalons Holy Regalia, now being used in a Christian mass. Enraged at this betrayal, Morgaine calls upon the Ladys magic, which results in the mysterious "disappearance" of the holy chalice, prompting the companions of the Round Table to embark on a 12-month quest to find it. Events spiral out of control when Lancelet returns, resumes his adulterous relationship with Gwenhwyfar, and is finally exposed. The novel closes with the King Stags death and Morgaines long-anticipated return to Avalon.</span>

    <a title="Karen’s Library" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7788355-karen-s-library"><img alt="Karen’s Library" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565022931p2/7788355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/280087411?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen’s Library" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen’s Library" href="/user/show/7788355-karen-s-library">Karen’s Library</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7788355-karen-s-library?shelf=re-read-quite-a-few-times">re-read-quite-a-few-times</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7788355-karen-s-library?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer280087411" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9480357720597312523">I think this is the first time I've ever liked the print book more than the audio. Although I love Davina Porter as narrator, because I couldn't skim over parts like I must have done when I read this book, it seemed a chore for me to finish this. I was completely over Gwenhwyfar and her whining. There seemed to be so much repeating and dragged out scenes. Overall, I love the storyline about the tale of King Arthur and his sister Morgaine. And I loved how the quest for the holy grail was interwov</span>
  <span id="freeText9480357720597312523" style="display:none">I think this is the first time I've ever liked the print book more than the audio. Although I love Davina Porter as narrator, because I couldn't skim over parts like I must have done when I read this book, it seemed a chore for me to finish this. I was completely over Gwenhwyfar and her whining. There seemed to be so much repeating and dragged out scenes. Overall, I love the storyline about the tale of King Arthur and his sister Morgaine. And I loved how the quest for the holy grail was interwoven into this old tale. The ending was peaceful, and explains how the pagans slowly vanished into the world of Christians. But I think I'm finally ready to say goodbye to The Mists of Avalon as it appears I've outgrown this book.</span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789722908450" href="/book/show/7294622-o-prisioneiro-da-rvore">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2208935-diana-marques-pap-is-e-letras?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2208935-diana-marques-pap-is-e-letras?shelf=on-paper">on-paper</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2208935-diana-marques-pap-is-e-letras?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2208935-diana-marques-pap-is-e-letras?shelf=arthurian-legend">arthurian-legend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2208935-diana-marques-pap-is-e-letras?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2208935-diana-marques-pap-is-e-letras?shelf=em-pt">em-pt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer320309195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3495510862829712927">This is the book that ends <i>the Mists of Avalon</i> series. This final book tells us of a Britain that starts to forget its pagan past and starts to assimilate in a stronger way the presence of the Christian's God. This is the conclusion of all plots and intrigues that were made up from the beggining of the series, and it also includes the already regular discussions about the pagan religion, the celtic one that gives importance to the women, and the present religion which asks for a submission to a </span>
  <span id="freeText3495510862829712927" style="display:none">This is the book that ends <i>the Mists of Avalon</i> series. This final book tells us of a Britain that starts to forget its pagan past and starts to assimilate in a stronger way the presence of the Christian's God. This is the conclusion of all plots and intrigues that were made up from the beggining of the series, and it also includes the already regular discussions about the pagan religion, the celtic one that gives importance to the women, and the present religion which asks for a submission to a male God in a patriarchal society. The narrative is beautifully constructed and, as I studied the origins of Arthurian legend and I am acquainted with the Divine Feminine concepts, I was curious to see how Marion Zimmer Bradley would end this. I loved her vision and the retelling of this legend and story, remembering the presence of the Goddess in Arthurian legend which is so important and is so often forgotten.<br /><br />I can say I loved pretty much everything in this book: from the presence of the Goddess in the legitimization of Arthur's power and authority, the violation of contract when it comes to the possession of Excalibur and the Holy Grail, the transformation of the Celtic myth into Christian myth... There are books in which we immerse ourselves in and this one, as well as the others in the series, was the case for me. The magic, the Arthurian story and characters, the focus on women and pagan beliefs, the <i>Mists of Avalon</i> series completely nailed it. The Arthurian myth fascinates me and I couldn't recommend this more to everyone who is curious about it or for those who want to have a different perspective on it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3495510862829712927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2338673872" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2338673872" />
    <a title="Inês Braz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47355196-in-s-braz"><img alt="Inês Braz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446474038p2/47355196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2338673872?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inês Braz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inês Braz" href="/user/show/47355196-in-s-braz">Inês Braz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7641478-as-brumas-de-avalon---o-prisioneiro-da-rvore">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2338673872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15237322650326161921">Once again... the series didn't survive a rereading. After years reading other tales about the Arthurian legend, I approached the Mists of Avalon series from a new angle. I have to admit that no other book made me wish so much the death of Arthur… it was really painful to watch/read. <br/><br/>About the characters… I don’t know if it was the author objective, but there’s not ONE male character that I liked in the story. Arthur was just… mushy, full of fussiness, and far from the figure of the rightful kin</span>
  <span id="freeText15237322650326161921" style="display:none">Once again... the series didn't survive a rereading. After years reading other tales about the Arthurian legend, I approached the Mists of Avalon series from a new angle. I have to admit that no other book made me wish so much the death of Arthur… it was really painful to watch/read. <br /><br />About the characters… I don’t know if it was the author objective, but there’s not ONE male character that I liked in the story. Arthur was just… mushy, full of fussiness, and far from the figure of the rightful king and honoured man that comes to mind in general when thinking about the character. He’s a pawn, a really silly and vulnerable to other people influences at that. <br /><br />Guinevere… well, she’s so full of bullshit that one, that it’s really hard to speak about her maintaining a certain composure. Gwenhwyfar, as she’s called in the books, is a silly girl, extremely religious, and no, she’s not the strong female character of the book. It was really hard to go true the states of mind of Gwen, when you understand that she doesn’t do anything meaningful in the story but just sits there like a mexican drama character whining about wanting to be with Lancelot but also her husband, and how to convert Arthur to Christianity just because… well, just because she has nothing else to do. The constant wining of the character makes you want to kill her constantly. <br /><br />Lancelot. Oh Lancelot… what have they done to you…<br />Lancelot is not a knight; he’s a pet, constantly waging his tail and run when called by Gwenhwyfar or Arthur. He have his moments of common sense, usually when he’s not in court (by far his best moments are when he’s in Avalon), but they don’t cover for all the lame moments of the story. <br /><br />Morgaine: she was sell as the strong female figure of the book. Well, all of the female characters where supposed to be the strong character from the story. That wasn’t the idea that I got rereading the story. She’s the lost character constantly trying to find herself, find the way to live her life in a changing world, but she’s kind of the Gwen from Avalon. She wines A LOT too, but gets things done, and someway she’s more loveable than her Christian counterpart. But then when you understand that she’s a Vivian wannabe but constantly goes to her emo state of mind, and in the end she’s one of the pawns also and not a player. Well, that takes a lot from the character.<br /><br />There are two characters in the book that, for me, where the strong strings: Vivian and Morgause. Those two where, for me, the real deal in the story. For different reasons, of course, but they kept the bar until the last time they appeared in the story. Those were the ones that made me kept reading, not Arthur, not Lancelot, not Morgaine. And certainly not Gwenhwyfar. <br />My favorite character by far was Taliesin, the first Merlin of Britain in the story. That sweet old man always had a way to speak to each one of the characters wisely, was kind but never dull. <br /><br />Maybe I’m now used to multifaceted characters in a way that I can no longer connect with those kind of wining characters; or maybe this is the king of book that should be read at a certain time of life (the first time I read it, I really liked it… years later it was quite different), but comparing to other tales, the Mists of Avalon came a bit short of the expected. The author keeps repeating herself times and times over, with the same words, the same excuses, and the same characters state of minds. It got tiring really early in the book. <br /><br />And that ending… let’s not start with it… I’m just glad that it ended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15237322650326161921" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3104614050?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carl Williams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carl Williams" href="/user/show/3191565-carl-williams">Carl Williams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3191565-carl-williams?shelf=historic-fiction">historic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3191565-carl-williams?shelf=books-for-a-free-day">books-for-a-free-day</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3104614050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5223836086032153275">I use to argue with my old anthropology professor about which was the best modern version of Camelot, the round table, and all that—she preferred the T. H. White _Once and Future King_ while I argued for Mary Stewart’s Merlin trilogy (plus two). And while it’s true _The Crystal Cave_ and the others, remain my favorite in fact I like the story of Arthur in most any form (I’ll even confess to enjoying the television series _Merlin__), the quest for capital “T” truth, the hero’s fatal flaw and all </span>
  <span id="freeText5223836086032153275" style="display:none">I use to argue with my old anthropology professor about which was the best modern version of Camelot, the round table, and all that—she preferred the T. H. White _Once and Future King_ while I argued for Mary Stewart’s Merlin trilogy (plus two). And while it’s true _The Crystal Cave_ and the others, remain my favorite in fact I like the story of Arthur in most any form (I’ll even confess to enjoying the television series _Merlin__), the quest for capital “T” truth, the hero’s fatal flaw and all that. Whether it’s Stewart’s tale of a slightly confused but earnest Merlin who’s never quite sure what he says in his prophecies or Bernard Cornwell’s _Warlord Chronicles_, a blood and guts warrior point-of-view it’s a story which is not only always adaptable but always relevant.<br /><br />I’m not sure what took me so long to get to read Marion Zimmer Bradley’s four novels centering on the women of Camelot. A good read, and I throughly enjoyed it. Without getting into too much detail, essentially it’s conflict laid out between an agoraphobic self-centered shrew who, while pretty, is very practiced at rationalizing her own behavior and a complex but sometimes reactive educated woman among women. It is really the story a conflict between cultures and belief systems, a wide open view of Celtic pagan goddess worship and a narrow stifling presentation of the new, Christian church. Good stuff and well done. If you haven’t read it, you might want to—and if you want to argue about which is the best version I’m happy to do that too. ;-)</span>
  <a data-text-id="5223836086032153275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1309040235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1309040235" />
    <a title="Guilherme Bahia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41152284-guilherme-bahia"><img alt="Guilherme Bahia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433612268p2/41152284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1309040235?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guilherme Bahia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guilherme Bahia" href="/user/show/41152284-guilherme-bahia">Guilherme Bahia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1309040235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3575505723493091168">(Abaixo, o comentário em português)<br/>In this last book of the series we see the demise of a once thriving spirituality, the despair for a world whose end is coming, the angst before the doubt: could it have been avoided?<br/><br/>But is it really the end? Is it merely the destruction of one faith by another? Will no feature of the defeated faith remain? The author beautifully explores the fact that Christianity was still in formation, and therefore not entirely closed to external influences. We then have so</span>
  <span id="freeText3575505723493091168" style="display:none">(Abaixo, o comentário em português)<br />In this last book of the series we see the demise of a once thriving spirituality, the despair for a world whose end is coming, the angst before the doubt: could it have been avoided?<br /><br />But is it really the end? Is it merely the destruction of one faith by another? Will no feature of the defeated faith remain? The author beautifully explores the fact that Christianity was still in formation, and therefore not entirely closed to external influences. We then have some key elements of Christian tradition presented as interesting cases of syncretism. The quest for the Holy Grail is one of them, in which we are also reminded of how eager people of that time were to believe in supernatural forces. The Virgin Mary is another one. In an epoch when eminent Christians like Archbishop of Constantinople Nestorius argued that making her the mother of God would mean that she herself was a goddess, Morgaine’s secret joy at Mary’s cult does not seem exaggerated.<br /><br />But let’s not get too optimistic. There is no happy end for Avalon, as we already know before reading the series. For all its flexibility in absorbing some pagan beliefs, the Christianity we see in the book is the one we know from the early Middle Ages: an enforcer of the totalitarian Augustinian society. The story then acquires a progressively depressing tone. The world we learn to like falls apart. And the more things went bad (may the Goddess forgive me), the more I liked the book! I can’t help myself: I love sad stories!<br /><br />============================================<br /><br />Neste ultimo livro da série nós vemos o ocaso de uma espiritualidade outrora pujante, o desespero por um mundo que desmorona, a angústia diante da dúvida: poderia ter sido diferente?<br /><br />Mas o que vemos é realmente o fim? É simplesmente a destruição de uma fé por outra? Nada restará da fé derrotada? A autora explora de maneira belíssima o fato de que o cristianismo ainda estava em formação, e portanto não totalmente fechado a influências externas. Vemos então alguns elementos chave da tradição cristã apresentados como interessantes casos de sincretismo. A busca pelo Santo Graal é um deles, no qual também somos lembrados do quanto as pessoas daquele tempo eram inclinadas a crer em forças sobrenaturais. A Virgem Maria é outro. Numa época em que cristãos eminentes como o arcebispo de Constantinopla argumentavam que fazer dela a mãe de Deus significaria que ela própria era uma deusa, a alegria secreta de Morgana pelo culto a Maria não parece exagerada.<br /><br />Mas vamos devagar com o otimismo. Não há final feliz para Avalon, como já sabemos antes de ler a série. Apesar de toda a sua flexibilidade para absorver elementos pagãos, o cristianismo mostrado no livro é aquele que nós conhecemos do começo da Idade Média: um braço da sociedade totalitária agostiniana. A história então adquire um tom progressivamente depressivo. O mundo que aprendemos a gostar entra em colapso. E quanto pior as coisas ficavam (que a Deusa me perdoe), mais eu gostava do livro! Não consigo resistir: eu adoro histórias tristes!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3575505723493091168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('617a3PMwoKxxSTQ9UIerUv8VS40WdSlJ0emKrAj4OcdUuk3k09DA3FpIVPqWaNNiggbyznOyoMw9U9CnQHA1uA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Historical examples range from the mid<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6988419181046747951\" style=\"display:none\">An accessible introduction to the relationship between the workers’ movement and the women’s movement, this book investigates the questions Why does gender inequality exist? and How does it relate to capitalism? Historical examples range from the mid-19th century to the 1970s and include events, debates, and key personalities from China, Russia, the United States, France, Italy, Spain, and Britain. It shows time and again the controversial, often difficult relationship between feminism and Marxism. The theoretical questions discussed include the origins of women’s oppression, domestic labor, dual systems theory, performativity, and differentialism. Women’s oppression is a structural element of the division of labor and one of the direct factors through which capitalism not only reinforces its ideological domination but also organizes the exploitation and reproduction of labor. The integration of patriarchal relations and capitalism has led to their radical transformation—in the family, in terms of women’s place in production, in sexual relations, and with respect to sexual identity. Marxism needs to probe complex processes: ongoing transformations and crises, a global context creating an increasingly feminized workforce, and changing relations between men and women. The book maintains that it is a mistake to submerge gender into class or to believe that freedom from exploitation automatically brings about women’s liberation and the ending of sexual roles; it is       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4841825.Marion_Zimmer_Bradley">About Marion Zimmer Bradley</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4841825.Marion_Zimmer_Bradley"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1305483488p3/4841825.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4841825.Marion_Zimmer_Bradley">Marion Zimmer Bradley
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11987529984655560988">Marion Eleanor Zimmer Bradley was an American author of fantasy novels such as <i>The Mists of Avalon</i> and the <i>Darkover</i> series, often with a feminist outlook.<br/><br/>Bradley's first published novel-length work was <i>Falcons of Narabedla</i>, first published in the May 1957 issue of <b>Other Worlds</b>. When she was a child, Bradley stated that she enjoyed reading adventure fantasy authors such as Henry Kuttner, Edmond Ham</span>
  <span id="freeText11987529984655560988" style="display:none">Marion Eleanor Zimmer Bradley was an American author of fantasy novels such as <i>The Mists of Avalon</i> and the <i>Darkover</i> series, often with a feminist outlook.<br /><br />Bradley's first published novel-length work was <i>Falcons of Narabedla</i>, first published in the May 1957 issue of <b>Other Worlds</b>. When she was a child, Bradley stated that she enjoyed reading adventure fantasy authors such as Henry Kuttner, Edmond Hamilton, and Leigh Brackett, especially when they wrote about "the glint of strange suns on worlds that never were and never would be." Her first novel and much of her subsequent work show their influence strongly.<br /><br />Early in her career, writing as Morgan Ives, Miriam Gardner, John Dexter, and Lee Chapman, Marion Zimmer Bradley produced several works outside the speculative fiction genre, including some gay and lesbian pulp fiction novels. For example, <i>I Am a Lesbian</i> was published in 1962. Though relatively tame by today's standards, they were considered pornographic when published, and for a long time she refused to disclose the titles she wrote under these pseudonyms.<br /><br />Her 1958 story <i>The Planet Savers</i> introduced the planet of Darkover, which became the setting of a popular series by Bradley and other authors. The Darkover milieu may be considered as either fantasy with science fiction overtones or as science fiction with fantasy overtones, as Darkover is a lost earth colony where psi powers developed to an unusual degree. Bradley wrote many Darkover novels by herself, but in her later years collaborated with other authors for publication; her literary collaborators have continued the series since her death.<br /><br />Bradley took an active role in science-fiction and fantasy fandom, promoting interaction with professional authors and publishers and making several important contributions to the subculture.<br /><br />For many years, Bradley actively encouraged Darkover fan fiction and reprinted some of it in commercial Darkover anthologies, continuing to encourage submissions from unpublished authors, but this ended after a dispute with a fan over an unpublished Darkover novel of Bradley's that had similarities to some of the fan's stories. As a result, the novel remained unpublished, and Bradley demanded the cessation of all Darkover fan fiction.<br /><br />Bradley was also the editor of the long-running <i>Sword and Sorceress</i> anthology series, which encouraged submissions of fantasy stories featuring original and non-traditional heroines from young and upcoming authors. Although she particularly encouraged young female authors, she was not averse to including male authors in her anthologies. Mercedes Lackey was just one of many authors who first appeared in the anthologies. She also maintained a large family of writers at her home in Berkeley. Ms Bradley was editing the final <i>Sword and Sorceress</i> manuscript up until the week of her death in September of 1999.<br /><br />Probably her most famous single novel is <i>The Mists of Avalon</i>. A retelling of the Camelot legend from the point of view of Morgaine and Gwenhwyfar, it grew into a series of books; like the Darkover series, the later novels are written with or by other authors and have continued to appear after Bradley's death.<br /><br />In 2000, she was posthumously awarded the World Fantasy Award for Lifetime Achievement. <br /><br />In 2014, Bradley was accused of sexual abuse by her daughter, Moira Greyland, who claims that she was molested from the age of 3 to 12. Greyland also claimed that she was not the only victim and that she was one of the people who reported her father, Walter H. Breen, for child molestation. In response to these allegations Bradley's publisher Victor Gollancz Ltd announced that they will donate all income from the sales of Bradley's e-books to the charity Save the Children.<br /><br />- <i>From Wikipedia</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11987529984655560988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Ainda que pouco ou nada se saiba sobre mitos, rituais m<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2600703138741747876\" style=\"display:none\">Fascínio é o termo exacto para caracterizar esta obra apaixonante baseada num tema que há séculos vem fazendo sonhar gerações e gerações: a lenda do Rei Artur e dos Cavaleiros da Távola Redonda. Ainda que pouco ou nada se saiba sobre mitos, rituais mágicos e esoterismo, deste livro ninguém sai intacto. Página após página, apodera-se do leitor um único desejo: o de continuar, mesmo pensando que se conhece o seguimento.\nUniverso paralelo a uma Grã-Bretanha celta, a misteriosa Ilha de Avalon é a guardiã dos grandes mistérios eternos e sagrados e os que estão fadados a existir nos dois mundos são, passo a passo, confrontados com as antigas tradições ligadas à Natureza e às suas forças obscuras e a nova fé cristã que procura espalhar-se no território e insinuar-se nos corações.\nA vetusta Matéria da Bretanha, com a sua trama de lealdade e pureza, bravura e traição, tão difundida na Europa medieval e que o nosso rei D. João I mostrou desejo de que fosse principal exemplo na formação de seus filhos, é apresentada nesta maravilhosa obra sob aspectos inesperados.\nReinvenção do ciclo de histórias contidas na lenda, este romance, impregnado de uma magia doce e bela, é narrado a partir do ponto de vista das mulheres que, por detrás do trono, foram as verdadeiras detentoras do poder: Morgaine e Gwynhefar.\nA misteriosa e cativante Morgaine é meia-irmã de Arthur e grã-sacerdotisa da brumosa Avalon, terra encantada que as mulheres governam pelo seu poder de gerar vida e onde o verdadeiro conhecimento é preservado para os vindouros. Para Morgaine existe um único objectivo vital: afastar a Bretanha da nova religião que encara a mulher como portadora do pecado original. Sua inimiga, a bela Rainha Gwynhefar vive dividida entre o dever para com o seu rei e o novo Deus e a paixão pelo valoroso Lancelet.\nAos dez anos, Marion Zimmer Bradley deixou-se enfeitiçar pela lenda do Rei Artur e nunca mais conseguiu desligar-se desse fascínio. Ao longo de muitos anos, pesquisou e estudou um imenso acervo documental sobre cristianismo primitivo, religião celta, sobre a Grã-Bretanha de antanho; conviveu com mulheres que nos nossos dias se consideram continuadoras e guardiãs das antigas tradições. Este volume e os que se lhe seguem são o seu tributo a um período histórico tão rico e pleno que não necessita de artificialidades para preencher a narrativa.\nIsaac Azimov considerou esta obra «absolutamente extraordinária» e tem razão: as lendas de sempre podem, de facto, transformar-se nos mais belos romances da actualidade.\nAs Brumas de Avalon foi dividido pela sua autora em quatro livros distintos que podem ser lidos individualmente sem que daí advenha quebra de ritmo para o leitor, pois cada um deles constitui um episódio independente e completo.<\/span>\n  <a       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/6517583-the-prisoner-in-the-oak">Quotes from The Prisoner in t...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;What sorrow is like to the sorrow of one who is alone?<br />Once I dwelt in the company of the king I loved well,<br />And my arm was heavy with the weight of the rings he gave,<br />And my heart weighed down with the gold of his love.<br />The face the king is like the sun to those who surrounded,.<br />But now my heart is empty<br />And I wander along throughout the world.<br />The groves take on their blossoms,<br />The trees and meadows grow fair<br />But the cuckoo, saddest of singers,<br />Cries forth the only sorrow of the exile,<br />And now my heart hoes wandering,<br />In search of what I shall never see more;<br />All faces are alike to me if I cannot see the face of my king,<br />And all countries are alike to me <br />When I cannot see the fair fields and meadows of my home.<br />So I shall arise and follow my heart in its wandering<br />For what is the fair meadow of home to me<br />When I cannot see the face of my king<br />And the weight on my arm is but a band of gold<br />When the heart is empty of the weight of love.<br />And so I shall go roaming <br />Over the fishers' road<br />And the road of the great whale <br />And beyond the country of the wave<br />With none to bear me company<br />But the memory of those I loved<br />And the songs I sang out of a full heart,<br />And the cuckoo's cry in memory.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/707710-what-sorrow-is-like-to-the-sorrow-of-one-who">4 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
